<p><b>Liège–Bastogne–Liège</b>, often called <i>La Doyenne</i> ("The Old Lady"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[N 1]</a></sup> is a one-day classic cycling race in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_March_1993_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_March_1993-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> First run in 1892, it is the oldest of the five <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_cycle_races#The_.27Monuments.27" title="Classic cycle races">Monuments</a></i> of the European professional <a href="/wiki/Road_bicycle_racing" title="Road bicycle racing">road cycling</a> calendar;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> usually coming as the last of the spring classics. It is held annually in late April, in the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes" title="Ardennes">Ardennes</a> region of Belgium, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge_(city)" title="Liège (city)">Liège</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bastogne" title="Bastogne">Bastogne</a> and back.</p>
<p>It is considered one of the most arduous one-day cycling events in the world because of its length and demanding course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-6">[5]</a></sup> The most successful rider with five victories is Belgian rider <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a>, trailed by Italian <a href="/wiki/Moreno_Argentin" title="Moreno Argentin">Moreno Argentin</a> who won four times in the 1980s. In recent times Spaniard <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Valverde" title="Alejandro Valverde">Alejandro Valverde</a> won the race three times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Liège–Bastogne–Liège is part of the <a href="/wiki/UCI_World_Tour" title="UCI World Tour">UCI World Tour</a> competition. It is the concluding race of the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes_classics" title="Ardennes classics">Ardennes Classics</a> series, which includes <a href="/wiki/La_Fl%C3%A8che_Wallonne" title="La Flèche Wallonne">La Flèche Wallonne</a>. Both are organised by French owner <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Sport_Organisation" title="Amaury Sport Organisation">Amaury Sport Organisation</a>, which also organises the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France" title="Tour de France">Tour de France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paris%E2%80%93Roubaix" title="Paris–Roubaix">Paris–Roubaix</a>.</p>
<p>As from 2017, a women's version of Liège–Bastogne–Liège will be held for the first time. The event will be included in the <a href="/wiki/2017_UCI_Women%27s_World_Tour" title="2017 UCI Women's World Tour">2017 UCI Women's World Tour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CN_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-CN-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VN_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-VN-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spa-Bastogne-Spa">Spa-Bastogne-Spa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spa-Bastogne-Spa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like many of cycling's classics, Liège–Bastogne–Liège was first organized by a newspaper (<i>L'Expresse</i>). Because the paper was published in French, aimed at a <a href="/wiki/French_Community_of_Belgium" title="French Community of Belgium">French-Belgian</a> audience, the route has always stayed in the southern, French-speaking part of Belgium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_April_2002_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_April_2002-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Houa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="288" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Houa.jpg/170px-Houa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Houa.jpg/255px-Houa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Houa.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Houa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Houa" title="Léon Houa">Léon Houa</a> won the first three editions of Liège–Bastogne–Liège in the late 19th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The race had its first running for amateurs in 1892, from <a href="/wiki/Spa,_Belgium" title="Spa, Belgium">Spa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bastogne" title="Bastogne">Bastogne</a> and back, over a 250 km distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_March_1993_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_March_1993-3">[2]</a></sup> As bicycles were expensive in the late 19th century, cycling was considered an exclusive sport for the wealthy, and the event was considered a "gentlemen's affair". 33 riders from the <i>Liège cycling union</i> and the <i>Pesant Club Liégois</i>, all Belgians and most of them from Liège, took the start. Only 17 finished. The course halfway turn point was the train station in Bastogne, chosen because of its convenience for race officials. Some tired riders abandoned the race in Bastogne and took the train back to Spa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_April_2002_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_April_2002-10">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Houa" title="Léon Houa">Léon Houa</a>, a Liège native, won the race after 10 hours and 48 minutes on the bike. The second-place finisher, Léon Lhoest, came in at 22 minutes, the third, Louis Rasquinet, at 44 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bikeraceinfo_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-bikeraceinfo-11">[10]</a></sup> Riders kept arriving for another five hours.</p>
<p>Houa won again the next year, over the same course, this time by a margin of a half hour. In 1894 the first race for professionals was held, and the average speed rose from 23.3 km/h (14.5 mph) to 25 km/h (16 mph). Houa concluded his third win, by seven minutes over Rasquinet. Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Garin" title="Maurice Garin">Maurice Garin</a>, who would later become the first winner of the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France" title="Tour de France">Tour de France</a>, finished fourth. After the inaugural three editions, the race was not organized for another 14 years, after which it was sometimes open only to amateurs and semi-professionals.</p>
<p>The race was resumed in 1908, with a start and finish in Liège for the first time. It was won by Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Trousselier" title="André Trousselier">André Trousselier</a>. In 1909 the winner, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eug%C3%A8ne_Charlier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eugène Charlier (page does not exist)">Eugène Charlier</a>, was disqualified because he had changed bikes. <a href="/wiki/Victor_Fastre" title="Victor Fastre">Victor Fastre</a> was declared winner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bikeraceinfo_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-bikeraceinfo-11">[10]</a></sup> The event was cancelled during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> but resumed in 1919. The race was mainly won by Belgians, but started to attract more riders from <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>, the bike-crazed northern part of Belgium, who began to dominate the event. Fleming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfons_Schepers" title="Alfons Schepers">Alfons Schepers</a> gained three victories in the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">Interwar period</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ardennes_Classic">Ardennes Classic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ardennes Classic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Liège–Bastogne–Liège had some interruptions during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, but was again a calendar-fixture as from 1945 and began to attract some of the stars of European cycling. In 1951 the race was added to the <a href="/wiki/Challenge_Desgrange-Colombo" title="Challenge Desgrange-Colombo">Challenge Desgrange-Colombo</a>, the competition that combined cycling's greatest races at the time. Swiss <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_K%C3%BCbler" title="Ferdinand Kübler">Ferdinand Kübler</a> won the race in 1951 and 1952. Belgian favourite <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Impanis" title="Raymond Impanis">Raymond Impanis</a> became the race's eternal runner-up, with four second places but never a victory.</p>
<p>In the late 1950s <a href="/wiki/Fred_De_Bruyne" title="Fred De Bruyne">Fred De Bruyne</a> won the race three times in his first three participations, equalling the former record of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Houa" title="Léon Houa">Houa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfons_Schepers" title="Alfons Schepers">Schepers</a>. In 1957 two riders were declared winner. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germain_Derijcke" title="Germain Derijcke">Germain Derijcke</a> was first over the finish line, but he had crossed a closed trainrail crossing. Derijcke had won by a three-minute lead and judges felt he had not gained that much time from illegally crossing the railway, thereby not disqualifying him. Officials compromised to promote second-place rider <a href="/wiki/Frans_Schoubben" title="Frans Schoubben">Frans Schoubben</a> to first as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Procycling.2C_UK.2C_May_2000_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Procycling.2C_UK.2C_May_2000-12">[11]</a></sup> In 1959 Liège–Bastogne–Liège became part of the <a href="/wiki/Super_Prestige_Pernod_International" title="Super Prestige Pernod International">Super Prestige</a>, successor of the Desgrange-Colombo competition and early precursor of the <a href="/wiki/UCI_World_Tour" title="UCI World Tour">UCI World Tour</a>, making the <i>Ardennes Classic</i> one of the main cycling events of the year.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="413" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg/220px-Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg/330px-Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eddy_Merckx_1966.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Record winner <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a> won Liège–Bastogne–Liège five times.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1969_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="1969 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">1969</a> began the era of cycling icon <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a>, who gained five victories, three of which consecutive, and a total of seven podium finishes. The <a href="/wiki/1971_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="1971 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">1971 race</a> was run in appalling conditions, with snow and cold ravaging the peloton. Merckx gained one of his most memorable victories. The Belgian attacked solo at 92 kilometers from the finish and soon had a five-minute advantage over his pursuiters. In a rare display, he suffered a sudden fatigue near the end and was joined by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Pintens" title="Georges Pintens">Georges Pintens</a>. Pintens failed to distance a tired Merckx, and Merckx managed to outsprint his fellow Belgian to his second victory in the classic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouvet_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bouvet-13">[12]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> In 1972 the finish moved to <a href="/wiki/Verviers" title="Verviers">Verviers</a>, 15 km from Liège, but because of fans' protest, this was a one-year occasion. The edition was again won by Merckx. In 1975 <i>The Cannibal</i> sealed his fifth and final victory, making him the sole record-holder of <i>La Doyenne</i>.</p>
<p>French cycling greatness <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Hinault" title="Bernard Hinault">Bernard Hinault</a> won the race twice, both times in harrowing weather conditions. In <a href="/wiki/1977_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="1977 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">1977</a> Hinault made a late escape from a six-strong group including a faltering Eddy Merckx; three years later he won the epic <a href="/wiki/1980_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="1980 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">contest of 1980</a> in torrential snowfall and glacial temperatures (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, Italian <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_cycle_races" title="Classic cycle races">classics</a></i> specialist <a href="/wiki/Moreno_Argentin" title="Moreno Argentin">Moreno Argentin</a> won the race four times, narrowly missing Merckx' record. Argentin also gained three victories in the sister classic <a href="/wiki/La_Fl%C3%A8che_Wallonne" title="La Flèche Wallonne">La Flèche Wallonne</a>, earning him the title of <i>King of the Ardennes</i> in his day.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finish_in_Ans">Finish in Ans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Finish in Ans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1990 the <i>Pesant Club Liégeois</i> partnered with the <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Sport_Organisation" title="Amaury Sport Organisation">Société du Tour de France</a>, the organizer of cycling's flagships the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France" title="Tour de France">Tour de France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paris%E2%80%93Roubaix" title="Paris–Roubaix">Paris–Roubaix</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> The partnership led to a more professional organization, resulting in a complete overhauling of the race course: the start and finish moved to different locations in Liège and five new climbs were included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1990s Italians <a href="/wiki/Michele_Bartoli" title="Michele Bartoli">Michele Bartoli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Bettini" title="Paolo Bettini">Paolo Bettini</a> continued a tradition of Italian victories in La Doyenne, with two wins each. In 1997 Bartoli and <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Jalabert" title="Laurent Jalabert">Laurent Jalabert</a> made a decisive breakaway on the climb of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_de_La_Redoute" title="Côte de La Redoute">La Redoute</a>, 40 km from the finish. Both riders worked together and Bartoli broke away from the Frenchman on the steep slopes in the final kilometer of the race. Jalabert, a specialist of the Ardennes races, finished second two years in a row but failed to win the <i>Doyenne</i>. In 1999 Bartoli sought a third consecutive win, but his effort was thwarted by young Belgian <a href="/wiki/Frank_Vandenbroucke_(cyclist)" title="Frank Vandenbroucke (cyclist)">Frank vandenbroucke</a> who controlled the race and stunned followers with his victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="1569" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg/220px-Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg/330px-Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg/440px-Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Li%C3%A8ge_Bastogne_Li%C3%A8ge_019.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Peloton in Liège–Bastogne–Liège <a href="/wiki/2007_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="2007 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">2007</a> near <a href="/wiki/Houffalize" title="Houffalize">Tavigny</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/2005_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="2005 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">2005</a> Kazakh <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vinokourov" title="Alexander Vinokourov">Alexander Vinokourov</a> and German <a href="/wiki/Jens_Voigt" title="Jens Voigt">Jens Voigt</a> broke away from the peloton 80 km from the end. Although the escape seemed unlikely to stay away in modern cycling, the two riders made it to the finish ahead of the peloton, with Vinokourov beating Voigt in the sprint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other memorable editions were the races of 2009 and 2010. In <a href="/wiki/2009_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="2009 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">2009</a> young <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourger</a> <a href="/wiki/Andy_Schleck" title="Andy Schleck">Andy Schleck</a> produced a solo breakaway to beat a strong field and win the race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/2010_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="2010 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">2010</a> Alexander Vinokourov concluded his second victory by outsprinting his breakaway companion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Kolobnev" title="Alexander Kolobnev">Alexander Kolobnev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> The victory was controversial, not only because Vinokourov had recently returned to cycling after a doping ban, but also because it was suggested he had 'bought' the victory. Swiss magazine <a href="/wiki/L%27Illustr%C3%A9" title="L'Illustré">L'Illustré</a> published e-mail correspondence between the winner and runner-up that suggests Vinokourov paid Kolobnev €100,000 not to contest the final sprint. Both riders were later charged with bribery by Belgian authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent years <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> allrounder <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Valverde" title="Alejandro Valverde">Alejandro Valverde</a> won three times, all three sprint-victories of a select group at the finish.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Route">Route</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Route">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_course">Present course</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Present course">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="892" data-file-width="621" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png/220px-Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png/330px-Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png/440px-Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge_2011_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Route map of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge" title="2011 Liège-Bastogne-Liège">2011 edition</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The route of Liège–Bastogne–Liège crosses the two eastern <a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">Walloon</a> provinces, <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge_(province)" title="Liège (province)">Liège (province)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg_(Belgium)" title="Luxembourg (Belgium)">Luxembourg</a>, from north to south and back. Its distance is more or less fixed at 250–260 km. The race starts in the center of <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge" title="Liège">Liège</a>, after which the course follows a straightforward 95 km (59 mi) route southwards to <a href="/wiki/Bastogne" title="Bastogne">Bastogne</a>, and a winding 163 km (101 mi) route back to Liège.</p>
<p>The second half of the course contains numerous climbs, such as the Stockeu, Haute-Levée, La Redoute, and Saint-Nicolas, before finishing in the Liège suburb of <a href="/wiki/Ans" title="Ans">Ans</a>. In the final 15 km of the race, the course makes a remarkable transition from the meadowy and agrarian landscapes in the Ardennes to the post-industrial urban scenery of Liège.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Course_changes">Course changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Course changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Until 1991 the race finished in Liège's city center, with a flat run-in to the finish. In 1992 the finish moved to the industrial suburb of <a href="/wiki/Ans" title="Ans">Ans</a>, on the northwestern side of the city. The steep <a href="/wiki/Saint-Nicolas,_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Saint-Nicolas, Liège">Côte de Saint Nicolas</a> was included in the final kilometres, along with a final climb to the finish in <a href="/wiki/Ans" title="Ans">Ans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archives_-_lesoir.be_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archives_-_lesoir.be-25">[24]</a></sup> The move implied profound changes to the character of the race, as climbers with a strong uphill-sprint in recent years often wait until the final stretches to launch their ultimate attack.</p>
<p>The route usually has some minor changes every year, with some climbs skipped and others added, but the traditional finale containing the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_de_La_Redoute" title="Côte de La Redoute">Côte de La Redoute</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=C%C3%B4te_de_la_Roche-aux-Faucons&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Côte de la Roche-aux-Faucons (page does not exist)">Côte de la Roche-aux-Faucons</a> and Côte de Saint Nicolas has remained a fixture since the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[N 2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_characteristics">Race characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Race characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demands">Demands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Demands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="500" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png/220px-Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png/330px-Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png/440px-Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liege-Bastogne-Liege_2012_profil.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Profile of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_Li%C3%A8ge-Bastogne-Li%C3%A8ge" title="2012 Liège-Bastogne-Liège">2012</a> edition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Liège–Bastogne–Liège is considered one of the most arduous one-day races in the world because of its length and succession of steep climbs. Every edition, about a dozen climbs – ranging in length, gradient and difficulty – are addressed, offering opportunities to attack. The British magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Cycling_Weekly" title="Cycling Weekly">Cycling Weekly</a></i> stated:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>In purely physical terms, this is probably the toughest classic: the climbs are long, most of them are pretty steep as well, and they come up with depressing frequency in the final kilometers.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_March_1993_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cycling_Weekly.2C_UK.2C_13_March_1993-3">[2]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Four-times winner <a href="/wiki/Moreno_Argentin" title="Moreno Argentin">Moreno Argentin</a> said:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Riders who win in Liège are what we call</i> fondisti <i>- men with a superior level of stamina. [The climb of] La Redoute is like the <a href="/wiki/Mur_de_Huy" title="Mur de Huy">Mur de Huy</a> in that it has to be tackled at pace, from the front of the peloton. The gradient is about 14 or 15 per cent, and it comes after 220 or 230 kilometers, so you don't have to be a genius to work out how tough it is. I remember that we used to go up with a maximum of 39 x 21 - it's not quite as steep as the Mur de Huy. A lot of riders mistakenly think you should attack on the hardest part, but in reality you hurt people on the slightly flatter section that comes after this.</i></dd>
<dd><i>Liège is a race of trial by elimination, where it's very unlikely that a breakaway can go clear and decide the race before the final 100 km [62 mi]. You need to be strong and at the same time clever and calculating — in this sense it's a complete test of a cyclist's ability.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climbs">Climbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climbs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Redoute.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/La_Redoute.jpg/220px-La_Redoute.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/La_Redoute.jpg/330px-La_Redoute.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/La_Redoute.jpg/440px-La_Redoute.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Redoute.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The foot of the Côte de La Redoute in <a href="/wiki/Aywaille" title="Aywaille">Aywaille</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most iconic hill is the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_de_La_Redoute" title="Côte de La Redoute">Côte de La Redoute</a>, the 2.1 km climb in <a href="/wiki/Aywaille" title="Aywaille">Aywaille</a> at an average gradient of 8.4% with slopes of over 20%. For a long time in the 1980s and 1990s La Redoute, at ca. 40 km from the finish, was the breaking point of the race and often the place where decisive breakaways were launched. In recent years the climb seems to have lost that particular role, as many riders are able to keep up with the pace on the climb and race favourites often wait until the last stages of the race to make a break.</p>
<p>In modern cycling, as in many bike races, the decisive stretches have evolved towards the final climbs of the day. The <a href="/wiki/Saint-Nicolas,_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Saint-Nicolas, Liège">Côte de Saint-Nicolas</a> is the last categorized climb of the race, with the top at 6 km from the finish in Ans. It is a precipitous and atypical climb because it is not part of the forested hills in the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes" title="Ardennes">Ardennes</a>, but located in the middle of the industrial suburbs of Liège along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meuse_(river)" title="Meuse (river)">Meuse</a> river.</p>
<p>Course changes are frequent from year to year. Climbs are sometimes cut or others included. These are the climbs in recent editions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weather">Weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Weather">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The weather in April is often unpredictable and the race has repeatedly been affected by harsh weather conditions. In 1919, 1957, 1980, and 2016 there was severe snowfall. The edition of 1980 was exceptionally hard: snow fell from the start and temperatures were near freezing point, leading commentators to call it <i>'Neige-Bastogne-Neige'</i> (Snow-Bastogne-Snow). <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Hinault" title="Bernard Hinault">Bernard Hinault</a> attacked with 80 km (50 mi) to go and finished nearly 10 minutes ahead.</p>
<p>A feature published by the British magazine, <i>Procycling</i> in 2000, described the infamous race:</p>
<dl>
<dd>A cold wind that blew across Belgium brought snow flakes and then a heavy fall within moments of the race starting. (...) Riders struggled on, with hands to faces to keep a view of the road. The race was an anonymous mass of plastic jackets and windcheaters. Spectators stood in goggles like upmarket snowmen, red-faced in the bitterness. Within the hour some teams had barely a man left on the road. They pulled out two dozen at a time, men like <a href="/wiki/Gianbattista_Baronchelli" title="Gianbattista Baronchelli">Gibi Baronchelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Saronni" title="Giuseppe Saronni">Giuseppe Saronni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucien_Van_Impe" title="Lucien Van Impe">Lucien Van Impe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Ren%C3%A9_Bernaudeau" title="Jean-René Bernaudeau">Jean-René Bernaudeau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Procycling.2C_UK.2C_May_2000_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Procycling.2C_UK.2C_May_2000-12">[11]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Bernard Hinault, the winner, was one of just 21 riders to finish the race of 174 starters. It took three weeks for proper movement to return to two fingers of his right hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Procycling.2C_UK.2C_May_2000_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Procycling.2C_UK.2C_May_2000-12">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Ardennes_Classics">The Ardennes Classics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Ardennes Classics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ardennes_classics" title="Ardennes classics">Ardennes classics</a></div>
<p>Liège–Bastogne–Liège is the concluding race of the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes_classics" title="Ardennes classics">Ardennes Classics</a> series, which includes <a href="/wiki/La_Fl%C3%A8che_Wallonne" title="La Flèche Wallonne">La Flèche Wallonne</a>. Both are organised by <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Sport_Organisation" title="Amaury Sport Organisation">ASO</a>.</p>
<p>The Flèche Wallonne ("Walloon Arrow"), although younger than Liège–Bastogne–Liège, was longtime considered the more prestigious event of the two Ardennes Classics, showing how prestige and importance of a race can sometimes change over time. At one time, Flèche Wallonne and Liège–Bastogne–Liège were run on successive days known as <i>Le Weekend Ardennais</i>, with Liège–Bastogne–Liège organized on Saturday and the Flèche Wallonne on Sunday.</p>
<p>Only seven riders have won both races in the same year: <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_K%C3%BCbler" title="Ferdinand Kübler">Ferdinand Kübler</a> twice (in 1951 and 1952), <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spaniard</a> <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Valverde" title="Alejandro Valverde">Alejandro Valverde</a> twice (2006 &amp; 2015), <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgians</a> <a href="/wiki/Stan_Ockers" title="Stan Ockers">Stan Ockers</a> (1955), <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a> (1972), and <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Gilbert" title="Philippe Gilbert">Philippe Gilbert</a> (2011) and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italians</a> <a href="/wiki/Moreno_Argentin" title="Moreno Argentin">Moreno Argentin</a> (1991) and <a href="/wiki/Davide_Rebellin" title="Davide Rebellin">Davide Rebellin</a> (2004).</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/2011_Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge" title="2011 Liège–Bastogne–Liège">2011</a> Belgian <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Gilbert" title="Philippe Gilbert">Philippe Gilbert</a> won Liège–Bastogne–Liège, completing a unique streak of classics victories in a span of ten days. Gilbert had previously won the <a href="/wiki/Brabantse_Pijl" title="Brabantse Pijl">Brabantse Pijl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amstel_Gold_Race" title="Amstel Gold Race">Amstel Gold Race</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_Fl%C3%A8che_Wallonne" title="La Flèche Wallonne">La Flèche Wallonne</a>, thus achieving a historic <i>quadruple</i> of victories in the hilly classics of April. Gilbert beat <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a> brothers <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A4nk_Schleck" title="Fränk Schleck">Fränck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andy_Schleck" title="Andy Schleck">Andy Schleck</a> in a sprint of three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li%C3%A8ge%E2%80%93Bastogne%E2%80%93Li%C3%A8ge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Doyenne</i> is the female form of <i>doyen</i>, a French word literally meaning "dean". In its metaphorical meaning it denotes the most respected or prominent person in a particular field, usually the most senior person. Hence, <i>doyenne</i> roughly translates as "the oldest, most respected lady".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Because of the composition of its immigrant population, and the many Italian flags hanging out the windows, Saint-Nicolas is nicknamed the "Italian hill".</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
